1 He lifted his left arm where the gold was , and she picked up a knife and scraped his skin so that no gold should be left sticking to it .
2 ` What have you done with the bridge ? '
3 said she .
4 And he lifted his right arm , from which she took the bridge , while the Bad One looked on , well pleased .
5 ` Be sure that he does not run away , ' chuckled he .
6 ` Boil some water , and get him ready for cooking , while I go and invite my friends the water-demons to the feast . '
7 The woman seized Ball-Carrier between her finger and thumb , and was going to carry him to the kitchen , when the boy spoke : ' I am very lean and small now , ' he said , ` hardly worth the trouble of cooking ; but if you were to keep me two days , and gave me plenty of food , I should get big and fat .
8 As it is , your friends the water-demons would think you meant to laugh at them , when they found that I was the feast . '
9 ` Well , perhaps you are right , ' answered the Bad One ; ' I will keep you for two days . '
10 And he went out to visit the water-demons .
11 Meanwhile the servant , whose name was Lung Woman , led him into a little shed , and chained him up to a ring in the wall .
12 But food was given him every hour , and at the end of two days he was as fat and big as a Christmas turkey , and could hardly move his head from one side to the other .
13 ` He will do now , ' said the Bad One , who came constantly to see how he was getting on . '
14 I shall go and tell the water-demons that we expect them to dinner to-night .
15 Put the kettle on the fire , but be sure on no account to taste the broth . '
16 Lung-Woman lost no time in obeying her orders .
17 She built up the fire , which had got very low , filled the kettle with water , and passing a rope which hung from the ceiling through the handle , swung it over the flames .
18 Then she brought in Ball-Carrier , who , seeing all these preparations , wished that as long as he was in the kettle the water might not really boil , though it would hiss and bubble , and also , that the spirits would turn the water into fat .
19 The kettle soon began to sing and bubble , and Ball Carrier was lifted in .
20 Very soon the fat which was to make the sauce rose to the surface , and Ball-Carrier , who was bobbing about from one side to the other , called out that Lung-Woman had better taste the broth , as he though that some salt should be added to it .
21 The servant knew quite well that her master had forbidden her to do any thing of the kind , but when once the idea was put into her head , she found the smell from the XXXXX so delicious that she unhooked a long ladle from the wall and plunged it into the kettle .	kettle		bubble|friends|kettle|kitchen|name|orders|sauce|skin|spirits|woman

2 ` What have you done with the bridge ? '
3 said she .
4 And he lifted his right arm , from which she took the bridge , while the Bad One looked on , well pleased .
5 ` Be sure that he does not run away , ' chuckled he .
6 ` Boil some water , and get him ready for cooking , while I go and invite my friends the water-demons to the feast . '

8 As it is , your friends the water-demons would think you meant to laugh at them , when they found that I was the feast . '
9 ` Well , perhaps you are right , ' answered the Bad One ; ' I will keep you for two days . '
10 And he went out to visit the water-demons .
11 Meanwhile the servant , whose name was Lung Woman , led him into a little shed , and chained him up to a ring in the wall .
12 But food was given him every hour , and at the end of two days he was as fat and big as a Christmas turkey , and could hardly move his head from one side to the other .
13 ` He will do now , ' said the Bad One , who came constantly to see how he was getting on . '
14 I shall go and tell the water-demons that we expect them to dinner to-night .
15 Put the kettle on the fire , but be sure on no account to taste the broth . '
16 Lung-Woman lost no time in obeying her orders .
17 She built up the fire , which had got very low , filled the kettle with water , and passing a rope which hung from the ceiling through the handle , swung it over the flames .
18 Then she brought in Ball-Carrier , who , seeing all these preparations , wished that as long as he was in the kettle the water might not really boil , though it would hiss and bubble , and also , that the spirits would turn the water into fat .
19 The kettle soon began to sing and bubble , and Ball Carrier was lifted in .
20 Very soon the fat which was to make the sauce rose to the surface , and Ball-Carrier , who was bobbing about from one side to the other , called out that Lung-Woman had better taste the broth , as he though that some salt should be added to it .
21 The servant knew quite well that her master had forbidden her to do any thing of the kind , but when once the idea was put into her head , she found the smell from the kettle so delicious that she unhooked a long ladle from the wall and plunged it into the XXXXX .	kettle		bubble|fat|feast|friends|kettle|laugh|preparations|side|surface|time

18 The kettle soon began to sing and bubble , and Ball Carrier was lifted in .
19 Very soon the fat which was to make the sauce rose to the surface , and Ball-Carrier , who was bobbing about from one side to the other , called out that Lung-Woman had better taste the broth , as he though that some salt should be added to it .
20 The servant knew quite well that her master had forbidden her to do any thing of the kind , but when once the idea was put into her head , she found the smell from the kettle so delicious that she unhooked a long ladle from the wall and plunged it into the kettle .

17 The kettle soon began to sing and bubble , and Ball Carrier was lifted in .
18 Very soon the fat which was to make the sauce rose to the surface , and Ball-Carrier , who was bobbing about from one side to the other , called out that Lung-Woman had better taste the broth , as he though that some salt should be added to it .
19 The servant knew quite well that her master had forbidden her to do any thing of the kind , but when once the idea was put into her head , she found the smell from the kettle so delicious that she unhooked a long ladle from the wall and plunged it into the kettle .
20 ` You will spill it all , if you stand so far off , ' said the boy ; ` why do n't you come a little nearer ? '
21 And as she did so he cried to the XXXXX to give him back his usual size and strength and to make the water scalding hot Then he gave the kettle a kick , which upset all the boiling water upon her , and jumping over her body he seized once more the gold and the bridge , picked up his club and bow and arrows , and after setting fire to the Bad One 's hut , ran down to the river , which he crossed safely by the help of the bridge .	spirits		account|bridge|broth|cooking|orders|side|spirits|surface|time|woman

1 And he lifted his right arm , from which she took the bridge , while the Bad One looked on , well pleased .
2 ` Be sure that he does not run away , ' chuckled he .
3 ` Boil some water , and get him ready for cooking , while I go and invite my friends the water-demons to the feast . '
4 The woman seized Ball-Carrier between her finger and thumb , and was going to carry him to the kitchen , when the boy spoke : ' I am very lean and small now , ' he said , ` hardly worth the trouble of cooking ; but if you were to keep me two days , and gave me plenty of food , I should get big and fat .
5 As it is , your friends the water-demons would think you meant to laugh at them , when they found that I was the feast . '
6 ` Well , perhaps you are right , ' answered the Bad One ; ' I will keep you for two days . '
7 And he went out to visit the water-demons .
8 Meanwhile the servant , whose name was Lung Woman , led him into a little shed , and chained him up to a ring in the wall .
9 But food was given him every hour , and at the end of two days he was as fat and big as a Christmas turkey , and could hardly move his head from one side to the other .
10 ` He will do now , ' said the Bad One , who came constantly to see how he was getting on . '
11 I shall go and tell the water-demons that we expect them to dinner to-night .
12 Put the kettle on the fire , but be sure on no account to taste the broth . '
13 Lung-Woman lost no time in obeying her orders .
14 She built up the fire , which had got very low , filled the kettle with water , and passing a rope which hung from the ceiling through the handle , swung it over the flames .
15 Then she brought in Ball-Carrier , who , seeing all these preparations , wished that as long as he was in the kettle the water might not really boil , though it would hiss and bubble , and also , that the spirits would turn the water into fat .
16 The kettle soon began to sing and bubble , and Ball Carrier was lifted in .
17 Very soon the fat which was to make the sauce rose to the surface , and Ball-Carrier , who was bobbing about from one side to the other , called out that Lung-Woman had better taste the broth , as he though that some salt should be added to it .
18 The servant knew quite well that her master had forbidden her to do any thing of the kind , but when once the idea was put into her head , she found the smell from the kettle so delicious that she unhooked a long ladle from the wall and plunged it into the kettle .
19 ` You will spill it all , if you stand so far off , ' said the boy ; ` why do n't you come a little nearer ? '
20 And as she did so he cried to the spirits to give him back his usual size and strength and to make the water scalding hot Then he gave the kettle a kick , which upset all the boiling water upon her , and jumping over her body he seized once more the gold and the bridge , picked up his club and bow and arrows , and after setting fire to the Bad One 's hut , ran down to the river , which he crossed safely by the help of the bridge .
21 The XXXXX , which was made of wood , was burned to the ground before the Bad One came back with a large crowd of water-demons .	hut		bow|bridge|dinner|fat|friends|hut|kind|ladle|thumb|time

10 Lung-Woman lost no time in obeying her orders .
11 She built up the fire , which had got very low , filled the kettle with water , and passing a rope which hung from the ceiling through the handle , swung it over the flames .
12 Then she brought in Ball-Carrier , who , seeing all these preparations , wished that as long as he was in the kettle the water might not really boil , though it would hiss and bubble , and also , that the spirits would turn the water into fat .
13 The kettle soon began to sing and bubble , and Ball Carrier was lifted in .
14 Very soon the fat which was to make the sauce rose to the surface , and Ball-Carrier , who was bobbing about from one side to the other , called out that Lung-Woman had better taste the broth , as he though that some salt should be added to it .
15 The servant knew quite well that her master had forbidden her to do any thing of the kind , but when once the idea was put into her head , she found the smell from the kettle so delicious that she unhooked a long ladle from the wall and plunged it into the kettle .
16 ` You will spill it all , if you stand so far off , ' said the boy ; ` why do n't you come a little nearer ? '
17 And as she did so he cried to the spirits to give him back his usual size and strength and to make the water scalding hot Then he gave the kettle a kick , which upset all the boiling water upon her , and jumping over her body he seized once more the gold and the bridge , picked up his club and bow and arrows , and after setting fire to the Bad One 's hut , ran down to the river , which he crossed safely by the help of the bridge .
18 The hut , which was made of wood , was burned to the ground before the Bad One came back with a large crowd of water-demons .
19 There was not a sign of anyone or anything , so he started for the river , where he saw Ball Carrier sitting quietly on the other side .
20 Then the Bad One knew what had happened , and after telling the water demons that there would be no feast after all , he called to Ball-Carrier , who was eating an apple . '
21 I know your XXXXX now , ' he said , ` and as you have ruined me , and I am not rich any more , will you take me as your servant ? '	name		account|club|dinner|friends|help|laugh|name|servant|size|wall

1 The woman seized Ball-Carrier between her finger and thumb , and was going to carry him to the kitchen , when the boy spoke : ' I am very lean and small now , ' he said , ` hardly worth the trouble of cooking ; but if you were to keep me two days , and gave me plenty of food , I should get big and fat .
2 As it is , your friends the water-demons would think you meant to laugh at them , when they found that I was the feast . '
3 ` Well , perhaps you are right , ' answered the Bad One ; ' I will keep you for two days . '
4 And he went out to visit the water-demons .
5 Meanwhile the servant , whose name was Lung Woman , led him into a little shed , and chained him up to a ring in the wall .
6 But food was given him every hour , and at the end of two days he was as fat and big as a Christmas turkey , and could hardly move his head from one side to the other .
7 ` He will do now , ' said the Bad One , who came constantly to see how he was getting on . '
8 I shall go and tell the water-demons that we expect them to dinner to-night .
9 Put the kettle on the fire , but be sure on no account to taste the broth . '
10 Lung-Woman lost no time in obeying her orders .
11 She built up the fire , which had got very low , filled the kettle with water , and passing a rope which hung from the ceiling through the handle , swung it over the flames .
12 Then she brought in Ball-Carrier , who , seeing all these preparations , wished that as long as he was in the kettle the water might not really boil , though it would hiss and bubble , and also , that the spirits would turn the water into fat .

1 The servant knew quite well that her master had forbidden her to do any thing of the kind , but when once the idea was put into her head , she found the smell from the kettle so delicious that she unhooked a long ladle from the wall and plunged it into the kettle .
2 ` You will spill it all , if you stand so far off , ' said the boy ; ` why do n't you come a little nearer ? '
3 And as she did so he cried to the spirits to give him back his usual size and strength and to make the water scalding hot Then he gave the kettle a kick , which upset all the boiling water upon her , and jumping over her body he seized once more the gold and the bridge , picked up his club and bow and arrows , and after setting fire to the Bad One 's hut , ran down to the river , which he crossed safely by the help of the bridge .
4 The hut , which was made of wood , was burned to the ground before the Bad One came back with a large crowd of water-demons .
5 There was not a sign of anyone or anything , so he started for the river , where he saw Ball Carrier sitting quietly on the other side .
6 Then the Bad One knew what had happened , and after telling the water demons that there would be no feast after all , he called to Ball-Carrier , who was eating an apple . '
7 I know your name now , ' he said , ` and as you have ruined me , and I am not rich any more , will you take me as your servant ? '
8 ` Yes , I will , though you have tried to kill me , ' answered Ball-Carrier , throwing the bridge across the water as he spoke .
9 But when the Bad One was in the midst of the stream , the boy wished it to become small ; and the Bad One fell into the water and was drowned , and the world was rid of him .

11 Bureau of Ethnology . -RSB-
12 How Ball-carrier Finished His Task After Ball-Carrier had managed to drown the Bad One so that he could not do any more mischief , he forgot the way to his grandmother 's house , and could not find it again , though he searched everywhere .
13 During this time he wandered into many strange places , and had many adventures ; and one day he came to a hut where a young girl lived .
14 He was tired and hungry and begged her to let him in and rest , and he stayed a long while , and the girl became his wife .
15 One morning he saw two children playing in front of the hut , and went out to speak to them .

7 ` Oh , well , let her try , ' replied Ball-Carrier .
8 ` It is not the first time people have wished to do that .
9 And here I am still , you see ! '
10 ` Be careful , ' said the wife , ` she is very cunning . '

14 I am told that you are more powerful than any man on earth , and as I am powerful too , let us try which is the strongest . '

1 But at this moment the sister-in-law came up .
2 ` How do you do , brother-in-law ?
3 I have heard of you so often that I am very glad to meet you .
4 I am told that you are more powerful than any man on earth , and as I am powerful too , let us try which is the strongest . '
5 ` That will be delightful , ' answered he .
6 ` Suppose we begin with a short race , and then we will go on to other things . '
7 ` That will suit me very well , ' replied the woman , who was a witch .
8 ` And let us agree that the one who wins shall have the right to kill the other . '
9 ` Oh , certainly , ' said Ball-Carrier ; ' and I do n't think we shall find a flatter course than the prairie itself -- no one knows how many miles it stretches .
10 We will run to the end and back again . '
11 This being settled they both made ready for the race , and Ball-Carrier silently begged the good spirits to help him , and not to let him fall into the hands of this wicked witch .
12 ` When the sun touches the trunk of that tree we will start , ' said she , as they both stood side by side .
13 But with the first step Ball-Carrier changed himself into a wolf and for a long way kept ahead .
14 Then gradually he heard her creeping up behind him , and soon she was in front .
15 So Ball-Carrier took the shape of a pigeon and flew rapidly past her , but in a little while she was in front again and the end of the prairie was in sight . '
16 A crow can fly faster than a pigeon , ' thought he , and as a crow he managed to pass her and held his ground so long that he fancied she was quite beaten .
17 The witch began to be afraid of it too , and putting out all her strength slipped past him .
18 Next he put on the shape of a hawk , and in this form he reached the bounds of the prairie , he and the witch turning homewards at the moment .
19 Bird after bird he tried , but every time the witch gained on him and took the lead .
20 At length the goal was in sight , and Ball-Carrier knew that unless he could get ahead now he would be killed before his own door , under the eyes of his wife .

18 And as they returned weeping to the hut they caught a glimpse of the ball rolling away down the path back to the old grandmother .
19 One of the sons sprang forward to stop it , for Ball-Carrier had often told them the tale of how it had helped him to cross the river , but it was too quick for him , and they had to content themselves with the war club and bow and arrows , which were put carefully away .
20 By-and-by some travellers came past , and the chief among them asked leave to marry Ball-Carrier 's daughter .
21 The mother said she must have a little XXXXX to think over it , as her daughter was still very young ; so it was settled that the man should go away for a month with his friends , and then come back to see if the girl was willing .	time		body|children|fatigue|grandmother|home|leave|path|time|travellers|witch

13 The witch came panting up , furious at having lost the race which she felt certain of winning ; and Ball-Carrier , who had by this time changed back into his own shape , struck her on the head and killed her .
14 For a long while Ball-Carrier was content to stay quietly at home with his wife and children , for he was tired of adventures , and only did enough hunting to supply the house with food .
15 But one day he happened to eat some poisonous berries that he had found in the forest , and grew so ill that he felt he was going to die .
16 ` When I am dead do not bury me in the earth , ' he said , ` but put me over there , among that clump of trees . '
17 So his wife and her three children watched by him as long as he was alive , and after he was dead they took him up and laid the body on a platform of stakes which they had prepared in the grove .
18 And as they returned weeping to the hut they caught a glimpse of the ball rolling away down the path back to the old grandmother .
19 One of the sons sprang forward to stop it , for Ball-Carrier had often told them the tale of how it had helped him to cross the river , but it was too quick for him , and they had to content themselves with the war club and bow and arrows , which were put carefully away .
20 By-and-by some travellers came past , and the chief among them asked leave to marry Ball-Carrier 's daughter .
21 The mother said she must have a little time to think over it , as her XXXXX was still very young ; so it was settled that the man should go away for a month with his friends , and then come back to see if the girl was willing .	daughter		arrows|ball|body|bow|crow|daughter|glimpse|grandmother|river|time

1 A crow can fly faster than a pigeon , ' thought he , and as a crow he managed to pass her and held his ground so long that he fancied she was quite beaten .
2 The witch began to be afraid of it too , and putting out all her strength slipped past him .
3 Next he put on the shape of a hawk , and in this form he reached the bounds of the prairie , he and the witch turning homewards at the moment .
4 Bird after bird he tried , but every time the witch gained on him and took the lead .
5 At length the goal was in sight , and Ball-Carrier knew that unless he could get ahead now he would be killed before his own door , under the eyes of his wife .
6 His eyes had grown dim from fatigue , his wings flapped wearily and hardly bore him along , while the witch seemed as fresh as ever .
7 What bird was there whose flight was swifter than his ?
8 Would not the good spirits tell him ?
9 Ah , of course he knew ; why had he not thought of it at first and spared himself all that fatigue ?
10 And the next instant a humming bird , dressed in green and blue , flashed past the woman and entered the house .
11 The witch came panting up , furious at having lost the race which she felt certain of winning ; and Ball-Carrier , who had by this time changed back into his own shape , struck her on the head and killed her .
12 For a long while Ball-Carrier was content to stay quietly at home with his wife and children , for he was tired of adventures , and only did enough hunting to supply the house with food .
13 But one day he happened to eat some poisonous berries that he had found in the forest , and grew so ill that he felt he was going to die .

15 So his wife and her three children watched by him as long as he was alive , and after he was dead they took him up and laid the body on a platform of stakes which they had prepared in the grove .

2 At length the goal was in sight , and Ball-Carrier knew that unless he could get ahead now he would be killed before his own door , under the eyes of his wife .
3 His eyes had grown dim from fatigue , his wings flapped wearily and hardly bore him along , while the witch seemed as fresh as ever .
4 What bird was there whose flight was swifter than his ?
5 Would not the good spirits tell him ?
6 Ah , of course he knew ; why had he not thought of it at first and spared himself all that fatigue ?
7 And the next instant a humming bird , dressed in green and blue , flashed past the woman and entered the house .
8 The witch came panting up , furious at having lost the race which she felt certain of winning ; and Ball-Carrier , who had by this time changed back into his own shape , struck her on the head and killed her .
9 For a long while Ball-Carrier was content to stay quietly at home with his wife and children , for he was tired of adventures , and only did enough hunting to supply the house with food .
10 But one day he happened to eat some poisonous berries that he had found in the forest , and grew so ill that he felt he was going to die .
11 ` When I am dead do not bury me in the earth , ' he said , ` but put me over there , among that clump of trees . '
12 So his wife and her three children watched by him as long as he was alive , and after he was dead they took him up and laid the body on a platform of stakes which they had prepared in the grove .
13 And as they returned weeping to the hut they caught a glimpse of the ball rolling away down the path back to the old grandmother .
14 One of the sons sprang forward to stop it , for Ball-Carrier had often told them the tale of how it had helped him to cross the river , but it was too quick for him , and they had to content themselves with the war club and bow and arrows , which were put carefully away .
15 By-and-by some travellers came past , and the chief among them asked leave to marry Ball-Carrier 's daughter .
16 The mother said she must have a little time to think over it , as her daughter was still very young ; so it was settled that the man should go away for a month with his friends , and then come back to see if the girl was willing .
17 Now ever since Ball-Carrier 's death the family had been very poor , and often could not get enough to eat .
18 One morning the girl , who had had no supper and no breakfast , wandered off to look for cranberries , and though she was quite near home was astonished at noticing a large hut , which certainly had not been there when last she had come that way .
19 No one was about , so she ventured to peep in , and her surprise was increased at seeing , heaped up in one corner , a quantity of food of all sorts , while a little robin redbreast stood perched on a beam looking down upon her .
20 ` It is my father , I am sure , ' she cried ; and the bird piped in answer .
21 From that day , whenever they wanted food they went to the XXXXX , and though the robin could not speak , he would hop on their shoulders and let them feed him with the food they knew he liked best .	hut		children|cranberries|forest|friends|hut|instant|spirits|supper|tale|woman

10 By-and-by some travellers came past , and the chief among them asked leave to marry Ball-Carrier 's daughter .
11 The mother said she must have a little time to think over it , as her daughter was still very young ; so it was settled that the man should go away for a month with his friends , and then come back to see if the girl was willing .
12 Now ever since Ball-Carrier 's death the family had been very poor , and often could not get enough to eat .
13 One morning the girl , who had had no supper and no breakfast , wandered off to look for cranberries , and though she was quite near home was astonished at noticing a large hut , which certainly had not been there when last she had come that way .
14 No one was about , so she ventured to peep in , and her surprise was increased at seeing , heaped up in one corner , a quantity of food of all sorts , while a little robin redbreast stood perched on a beam looking down upon her .
15 ` It is my father , I am sure , ' she cried ; and the bird piped in answer .
16 From that day , whenever they wanted food they went to the hut , and though the robin could not speak , he would hop on their shoulders and let them feed him with the food they knew he liked best .
17 When the man came back he found the girl looking so much prettier and fatter than when he had left her , that he insisted that they should be married on the spot .
18 And the mother , who did not know how to get rid of him , gave in .
19 The husband spent all his time in hunting , and the family had never had so much meat before ; but the man , who had seen for himself how poor they were , noticed with amazement that they did not seem to care about it , or to be hungry .
20 ` They must get food from somewhere , ' he thought , and one morning , when he pretended to be going out to hunt , he hid in a thicket to watch .
21 Very soon they all left the house together , and walked to the other XXXXX , which the girl 's husband saw for the first time , as it was hid in a hollow .	hut		adventures|body|cranberries|forest|hut|river|shape|supper|time|travellers

1 Ah , of course he knew ; why had he not thought of it at first and spared himself all that fatigue ?
2 And the next instant a humming bird , dressed in green and blue , flashed past the woman and entered the house .
3 The witch came panting up , furious at having lost the race which she felt certain of winning ; and Ball-Carrier , who had by this time changed back into his own shape , struck her on the head and killed her .
4 For a long while Ball-Carrier was content to stay quietly at home with his wife and children , for he was tired of adventures , and only did enough hunting to supply the house with food .
5 But one day he happened to eat some poisonous berries that he had found in the forest , and grew so ill that he felt he was going to die .
6 ` When I am dead do not bury me in the earth , ' he said , ` but put me over there , among that clump of trees . '
7 So his wife and her three children watched by him as long as he was alive , and after he was dead they took him up and laid the body on a platform of stakes which they had prepared in the grove .
8 And as they returned weeping to the hut they caught a glimpse of the ball rolling away down the path back to the old grandmother .
9 One of the sons sprang forward to stop it , for Ball-Carrier had often told them the tale of how it had helped him to cross the river , but it was too quick for him , and they had to content themselves with the war club and bow and arrows , which were put carefully away .
10 By-and-by some travellers came past , and the chief among them asked leave to marry Ball-Carrier 's daughter .
11 The mother said she must have a little time to think over it , as her daughter was still very young ; so it was settled that the man should go away for a month with his friends , and then come back to see if the girl was willing .
12 Now ever since Ball-Carrier 's death the family had been very poor , and often could not get enough to eat .

18 The next day the husband declared that he was very ill , and could not eat anything ; but this was only a presence so that he might get what he wanted .
19 The family were all much distressed , and begged him to tell them what food he fancied .
20 ` Oh !

1 So his wife and her three children watched by him as long as he was alive , and after he was dead they took him up and laid the body on a platform of stakes which they had prepared in the grove .
2 And as they returned weeping to the hut they caught a glimpse of the ball rolling away down the path back to the old grandmother .
3 One of the sons sprang forward to stop it , for Ball-Carrier had often told them the tale of how it had helped him to cross the river , but it was too quick for him , and they had to content themselves with the war club and bow and arrows , which were put carefully away .
4 By-and-by some travellers came past , and the chief among them asked leave to marry Ball-Carrier 's daughter .
5 The mother said she must have a little time to think over it , as her daughter was still very young ; so it was settled that the man should go away for a month with his friends , and then come back to see if the girl was willing .
6 Now ever since Ball-Carrier 's death the family had been very poor , and often could not get enough to eat .
7 One morning the girl , who had had no supper and no breakfast , wandered off to look for cranberries , and though she was quite near home was astonished at noticing a large hut , which certainly had not been there when last she had come that way .
8 No one was about , so she ventured to peep in , and her surprise was increased at seeing , heaped up in one corner , a quantity of food of all sorts , while a little robin redbreast stood perched on a beam looking down upon her .
9 ` It is my father , I am sure , ' she cried ; and the bird piped in answer .
10 From that day , whenever they wanted food they went to the hut , and though the robin could not speak , he would hop on their shoulders and let them feed him with the food they knew he liked best .
11 When the man came back he found the girl looking so much prettier and fatter than when he had left her , that he insisted that they should be married on the spot .
12 And the mother , who did not know how to get rid of him , gave in .
13 The husband spent all his time in hunting , and the family had never had so much meat before ; but the man , who had seen for himself how poor they were , noticed with amazement that they did not seem to care about it , or to be hungry .
14 ` They must get food from somewhere , ' he thought , and one morning , when he pretended to be going out to hunt , he hid in a thicket to watch .
15 Very soon they all left the house together , and walked to the other hut , which the girl 's husband saw for the first time , as it was hid in a hollow .
16 He followed , and noticed that each one went up to the redbreast , and shook him by the claw ; and he then entered boldly and shook the bird 's claw too .
17 The whole party afterwards sat down to dinner , after which they all returned to their own hut .
18 The next day the husband declared that he was very ill , and could not eat anything ; but this was only a presence so that he might get what he wanted .
19 The family were all much distressed , and begged him to tell them what food he fancied .
20 ` Oh !

20 ` Well , then , I think , if I had that -- redbreast , nicely roasted , I could eat a little bit of his wing ! '

5 The whole party afterwards sat down to dinner , after which they all returned to their own hut .

7 The family were all much distressed , and begged him to tell them what food he fancied .
8 ` Oh !
9 I could not eat any food , ' he answered every time , and at each answer his voice grew fainter and fainter , till they thought he would die from weakness before their eyes .
10 ` There must be some thing you could take , if you would only say what it is , ' implored his wife .
11 ` No , nothing , nothing ; except , perhaps -- but of course that is impossible ! '
12 ` No , I am sure it is not , ' replied she ; ` you shall have it , I promise -- only tell me what it is . ' '
13 I think -- but I could not ask you to do such a thing .
14 Leave me alone , and let me die quietly . '
15 ` You shall not die , ' cried the girl , who was very fond of her husband , for he did not beat her as most girls ' husbands did .
16 ` Whatever it is , I will manage to get it for you . '
17 ` Well , then , I think , if I had that -- redbreast , nicely roasted , I could eat a little bit of his wing ! '
18 The wife started back in horror at such a request ; but the man turned his face to the wall , and took no notice , as he thought it was better to leave her to herself for a little .
19 Weeping and wringing her hands , the girl went down to her mother .
20 The brothers were very angry when they heard the story , and declared that , if any one were to die , it certainly should not be the robin .
21 But all that night the man seemed getting weaker and weaker , and at last , quite early , the wife crept out , and stealing to the hut , killed the XXXXX , and brought him home to her husband .	bird		anything|bird|course|face|girls|presence|robin|story|voice|wing

6 ` You shall not die , ' cried the girl , who was very fond of her husband , for he did not beat her as most girls ' husbands did .
7 ` Whatever it is , I will manage to get it for you . '
8 ` Well , then , I think , if I had that -- redbreast , nicely roasted , I could eat a little bit of his wing ! '
9 The wife started back in horror at such a request ; but the man turned his face to the wall , and took no notice , as he thought it was better to leave her to herself for a little .
10 Weeping and wringing her hands , the girl went down to her mother .
11 The brothers were very angry when they heard the story , and declared that , if any one were to die , it certainly should not be the robin .
12 But all that night the man seemed getting weaker and weaker , and at last , quite early , the wife crept out , and stealing to the hut , killed the bird , and brought him home to her husband .
13 Just as she was going to cook it her two brothers came in .
14 They cried out in horror at the sight , and , rushing out of the hut , declared they would never see her any more .
15 And the poor girl , with a heavy heart , took the body of the redbreast up to her husband .
16 But directly she entered the room the man told her that he felt a great deal better , and that he would rather have a piece of bear 's flesh , well boiled , than any bird , however tender .
17 His wife felt very miserable to think that their beloved redbreast had been sacrificed for nothing , and begged him to try a little bit .
18 ` You felt so sure that it would do you good before , ' said she , ` that I ca n't help thinking it would quite cure you now . '
19 But the man only flew into a rage , and flung the bird out of the window .
20 Then he got up and went out .
21 Now all this while the ball had been rolling , rolling , rolling to the old grandmother 's XXXXX on the other side of the world , and directly it rolled into her hut she knew that her grandson must be dead .	hut		bit|hands|heart|horror|hut|nothing|rage|room|sight|story

11 ` You felt so sure that it would do you good before , ' said she , ` that I ca n't help thinking it would quite cure you now . '
12 But the man only flew into a rage , and flung the bird out of the window .
13 Then he got up and went out .
14 Now all this while the ball had been rolling , rolling , rolling to the old grandmother 's hut on the other side of the world , and directly it rolled into her hut she knew that her grandson must be dead .
15 Without wasting any time she took a fox skin and tied it round her forehead , and fastened another round her waist , as witches always do when they leave their own homes .
16 When she was ready she said to the ball : ` Go back the way you came , and lead me to my grandson . '
17 And the ball started with the old woman following .
18 It was a long journey , even for a witch , but , like other things , it ended at last ; and the old woman stood before the platform of stakes , where the body of Ball-Carrier lay .
19 ` Wake up , my grandson , it is time to go home , ' the witch said .
20 And Ball-Carrier stepped down oft the platform , and brought his club and bow and arrows out of the hut , and set out , for the other side of the world , behind the old woman .
21 When they reached the hut where Ball-Carrier had fasted so many years ago , the old XXXXX spoke for the first time since they had started on their way .	woman		ball|body|club|deal|journey|notice|robin|story|wing|woman

2 Without wasting any time she took a fox skin and tied it round her forehead , and fastened another round her waist , as witches always do when they leave their own homes .
3 When she was ready she said to the ball : ` Go back the way you came , and lead me to my grandson . '
4 And the ball started with the old woman following .
5 It was a long journey , even for a witch , but , like other things , it ended at last ; and the old woman stood before the platform of stakes , where the body of Ball-Carrier lay .
6 ` Wake up , my grandson , it is time to go home , ' the witch said .
7 And Ball-Carrier stepped down oft the platform , and brought his club and bow and arrows out of the hut , and set out , for the other side of the world , behind the old woman .
8 When they reached the hut where Ball-Carrier had fasted so many years ago , the old woman spoke for the first time since they had started on their way .

10 ` Yes , grandmother , I got it . '
11 ` Where is it ? '
12 she asked .

5 After she had finished she asked again : ` My grandson , did you manage to get that bridge from the Bad One ? '
6 ` Yes , grandmother , I got that too , ' answered he .
7 ` Where is it ? '
8 she asked , and Ball-Carrier lifted his right arm , and pointed to his arm-pit .
9 ` Here is the bridge , grandmother , ' said he .
10 Then the witch did something that nobody in the world could have guessed that she would do .
11 First , she took the gold and said to Ball-carrier : ` My grandson , this gold must be hidden in the earth , for if people think they can get it when they choose , they will become lazy and stupid .
12 But if we take it and bury it in different parts of the world they will have to work for it if they want it , and then will only find a little at a time . '
13 And as she spoke , she pulled up one of the poles of the hut , and Ball-Carrier saw that underneath was a deep , deep hole , which seemed to have no bottom .
14 Down this hole she poured all the gold , and when it was out of sight it ran about all over the world , where people that dig hard sometimes find it .
15 And after that was done she put the pole back again .
16 Next she lifted down a spade from a high shelf , where it had grown quite rusty , and dug a very small hole on the opposite side of the hut -- very small , but very deep .
17 ` Give me the bridge , ' said she , ` for I am going to bury it here .
18 If anyone was to get hold of it , and find that they could cross rivers and seas without any trouble , they would never discover how to cross them for themselves .
19 I am a witch , and if I had chosen I could easily have cast my spells over the Bad One , and have made him deliver them to you the first day you came into my hut .
20 But then you would never have fasted , and never have planned how to get what you wanted , and never have known the good spirits , and would have been fat and idle to the end of your days .
21 And now go ; in that XXXXX , which you can just see far away , live your father and mother , who are old people now , and need a son to hunt for them .	hut		hold|hole|hut|pole|rivers|seas|spade|spirits|time|world

18 The Bunyip Long , long ago , far , far away on the other side of the world , some young men left the camp where they lived to get some food for their wives and children .

17 The Bunyip Long , long ago , far , far away on the other side of the world , some young men left the camp where they lived to get some food for their wives and children .
18 The sun was hot , but they liked heat , and as they went they ran races and tried who could hurl his spear the farthest , or was cleverest in throwing a strange weapon called a boomerang , which always returns to the thrower .
19 They did not get on very fast at this rate , but presently they reached a flat place that in time of flood was full of water , but was now , in the height of summer , only a set of pools , each surrounded with a fringe of plants , with bulrushes standing in the inside of all .
20 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
21 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the XXXXX and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?	water		anyone|boomerang|gold|onions|parts|pole|rate|side|sun|water

16 The Bunyip Long , long ago , far , far away on the other side of the world , some young men left the camp where they lived to get some food for their wives and children .
17 The sun was hot , but they liked heat , and as they went they ran races and tried who could hurl his spear the farthest , or was cleverest in throwing a strange weapon called a boomerang , which always returns to the thrower .
18 They did not get on very fast at this rate , but presently they reached a flat place that in time of flood was full of water , but was now , in the height of summer , only a set of pools , each surrounded with a fringe of plants , with bulrushes standing in the inside of all .
19 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
20 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the water and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?

6 I am a witch , and if I had chosen I could easily have cast my spells over the Bad One , and have made him deliver them to you the first day you came into my hut .
7 But then you would never have fasted , and never have planned how to get what you wanted , and never have known the good spirits , and would have been fat and idle to the end of your days .
8 And now go ; in that hut , which you can just see far away , live your father and mother , who are old people now , and need a son to hunt for them .
9 You have done what you were set to do , and I need you no more . '
10 Then Ball-Carrier remembered his parents and went back to them .
11 -LSB- From Bureau of Ethnology .
12 ` Indian Folklore . ' -RSB-
13 The Bunyip Long , long ago , far , far away on the other side of the world , some young men left the camp where they lived to get some food for their wives and children .
14 The sun was hot , but they liked heat , and as they went they ran races and tried who could hurl his spear the farthest , or was cleverest in throwing a strange weapon called a boomerang , which always returns to the thrower .
15 They did not get on very fast at this rate , but presently they reached a flat place that in time of flood was full of water , but was now , in the height of summer , only a set of pools , each surrounded with a fringe of plants , with bulrushes standing in the inside of all .
16 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
17 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the water and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?
18 Let them come and get the roots for themselves ; but we will fish for eels and anything else we can get . '
19 This delighted the rest of the party , and they all began to arrange their fishing lines , made from the bark of the yellow mimosa , and to search for bait for their hooks .
20 Most of them used worms , but one , who had put a piece of raw meat for dinner into his skin wallet , cut off a little bit and baited his line with it , unseen by his companions .
21 For a long XXXXX they cast patiently , without receiving a single bite ; the sun had grown low in the sky , and it seemed as if they would have to go home empty-handed , not even with a basket of roots to show ; when the youth , who had baited his hook with raw meat , suddenly saw his line disappear under the water .	time		heat|mother|pole|races|rest|side|spirits|time|tops|trouble

1 Down this hole she poured all the gold , and when it was out of sight it ran about all over the world , where people that dig hard sometimes find it .
2 And after that was done she put the pole back again .
3 Next she lifted down a spade from a high shelf , where it had grown quite rusty , and dug a very small hole on the opposite side of the hut -- very small , but very deep .
4 ` Give me the bridge , ' said she , ` for I am going to bury it here .
5 If anyone was to get hold of it , and find that they could cross rivers and seas without any trouble , they would never discover how to cross them for themselves .
6 I am a witch , and if I had chosen I could easily have cast my spells over the Bad One , and have made him deliver them to you the first day you came into my hut .
7 But then you would never have fasted , and never have planned how to get what you wanted , and never have known the good spirits , and would have been fat and idle to the end of your days .
8 And now go ; in that hut , which you can just see far away , live your father and mother , who are old people now , and need a son to hunt for them .
9 You have done what you were set to do , and I need you no more . '
10 Then Ball-Carrier remembered his parents and went back to them .

8 ` Indian Folklore . ' -RSB-
9 The Bunyip Long , long ago , far , far away on the other side of the world , some young men left the camp where they lived to get some food for their wives and children .
10 The sun was hot , but they liked heat , and as they went they ran races and tried who could hurl his spear the farthest , or was cleverest in throwing a strange weapon called a boomerang , which always returns to the thrower .
11 They did not get on very fast at this rate , but presently they reached a flat place that in time of flood was full of water , but was now , in the height of summer , only a set of pools , each surrounded with a fringe of plants , with bulrushes standing in the inside of all .
12 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
13 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the water and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?
14 Let them come and get the roots for themselves ; but we will fish for eels and anything else we can get . '
15 This delighted the rest of the party , and they all began to arrange their fishing lines , made from the bark of the yellow mimosa , and to search for bait for their hooks .
16 Most of them used worms , but one , who had put a piece of raw meat for dinner into his skin wallet , cut off a little bit and baited his line with it , unseen by his companions .
17 For a long time they cast patiently , without receiving a single bite ; the sun had grown low in the sky , and it seemed as if they would have to go home empty-handed , not even with a basket of roots to show ; when the youth , who had baited his hook with raw meat , suddenly saw his line disappear under the water .
18 Something , a very heavy fish he supposed , was pulling so hard that he could hardly keep his feet , and for a few minutes it seemed either as if he must let go or be dragged into the pool .
19 He cried to his friends to help him , and at last , trembling with fright at what they were going to see , they managed between them to land on the bank a creature that was neither a calf nor a seal , but something of both , with a long , broad tail .
20 They looked at each other with horror , cold shivers running down their spines ; for though they had never beheld it , there was not a man amongst them who did not know what it was -- the cub of the awful Bunyip !
21 All of a sudden the silence was broken by a low wail , answered by another from the other XXXXX of the pool , as the mother rose up from her den and came towards them , rage flashing from her horrible yellow eyes .	side		basket|camp|friends|side|sky|tail|tops|water|weapon|wives

15 This delighted the rest of the party , and they all began to arrange their fishing lines , made from the bark of the yellow mimosa , and to search for bait for their hooks .

4 The Bunyip Long , long ago , far , far away on the other side of the world , some young men left the camp where they lived to get some food for their wives and children .
5 The sun was hot , but they liked heat , and as they went they ran races and tried who could hurl his spear the farthest , or was cleverest in throwing a strange weapon called a boomerang , which always returns to the thrower .
6 They did not get on very fast at this rate , but presently they reached a flat place that in time of flood was full of water , but was now , in the height of summer , only a set of pools , each surrounded with a fringe of plants , with bulrushes standing in the inside of all .
7 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
8 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the water and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?
9 Let them come and get the roots for themselves ; but we will fish for eels and anything else we can get . '
10 This delighted the rest of the party , and they all began to arrange their fishing lines , made from the bark of the yellow mimosa , and to search for bait for their hooks .
11 Most of them used worms , but one , who had put a piece of raw meat for dinner into his skin wallet , cut off a little bit and baited his line with it , unseen by his companions .
12 For a long time they cast patiently , without receiving a single bite ; the sun had grown low in the sky , and it seemed as if they would have to go home empty-handed , not even with a basket of roots to show ; when the youth , who had baited his hook with raw meat , suddenly saw his line disappear under the water .
13 Something , a very heavy fish he supposed , was pulling so hard that he could hardly keep his feet , and for a few minutes it seemed either as if he must let go or be dragged into the pool .
14 He cried to his friends to help him , and at last , trembling with fright at what they were going to see , they managed between them to land on the bank a creature that was neither a calf nor a seal , but something of both , with a long , broad tail .
15 They looked at each other with horror , cold shivers running down their spines ; for though they had never beheld it , there was not a man amongst them who did not know what it was -- the cub of the awful Bunyip !
16 All of a sudden the silence was broken by a low wail , answered by another from the other side of the pool , as the mother rose up from her den and came towards them , rage flashing from her horrible yellow eyes .
17 ` Let it go !
18 let it go ! '
19 whispered the young men to each other ; but the captor declared that he had caught it , and was going to keep it .
20 ` He had promised his sweetheart , ' he said , ` that he would bring back enough meat for her father 's house to feast on for three days , and though they could not eat the little Bunyip , her brothers and sisters should have it to play with . '
21 So , flinging his XXXXX at the mother to keep her back , he threw the little Bunyip on to his shoulders , and set out for the camp , never heeding the poor mother 's cries of distress .	spear		anything|basket|food|heat|place|rate|side|spear|water|worms

6 They did not get on very fast at this rate , but presently they reached a flat place that in time of flood was full of water , but was now , in the height of summer , only a set of pools , each surrounded with a fringe of plants , with bulrushes standing in the inside of all .
7 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
8 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the water and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?
9 Let them come and get the roots for themselves ; but we will fish for eels and anything else we can get . '
10 This delighted the rest of the party , and they all began to arrange their fishing lines , made from the bark of the yellow mimosa , and to search for bait for their hooks .
11 Most of them used worms , but one , who had put a piece of raw meat for dinner into his skin wallet , cut off a little bit and baited his line with it , unseen by his companions .
12 For a long time they cast patiently , without receiving a single bite ; the sun had grown low in the sky , and it seemed as if they would have to go home empty-handed , not even with a basket of roots to show ; when the youth , who had baited his hook with raw meat , suddenly saw his line disappear under the water .
13 Something , a very heavy fish he supposed , was pulling so hard that he could hardly keep his feet , and for a few minutes it seemed either as if he must let go or be dragged into the pool .
14 He cried to his friends to help him , and at last , trembling with fright at what they were going to see , they managed between them to land on the bank a creature that was neither a calf nor a seal , but something of both , with a long , broad tail .
15 They looked at each other with horror , cold shivers running down their spines ; for though they had never beheld it , there was not a man amongst them who did not know what it was -- the cub of the awful Bunyip !
16 All of a sudden the silence was broken by a low wail , answered by another from the other side of the pool , as the mother rose up from her den and came towards them , rage flashing from her horrible yellow eyes .
17 ` Let it go !
18 let it go ! '
19 whispered the young men to each other ; but the captor declared that he had caught it , and was going to keep it .
20 ` He had promised his sweetheart , ' he said , ` that he would bring back enough meat for her father 's house to feast on for three days , and though they could not eat the little Bunyip , her brothers and sisters should have it to play with . '
21 So , flinging his spear at the mother to keep her back , he threw the little Bunyip on to his shoulders , and set out for the camp , never heeding the poor XXXXX 's cries of distress .	mother		boomerang|camp|children|den|food|friends|mother|rate|spear|world

1 -LSB- From Bureau of Ethnology .
2 ` Indian Folklore . ' -RSB-
3 The Bunyip Long , long ago , far , far away on the other side of the world , some young men left the camp where they lived to get some food for their wives and children .
4 The sun was hot , but they liked heat , and as they went they ran races and tried who could hurl his spear the farthest , or was cleverest in throwing a strange weapon called a boomerang , which always returns to the thrower .
5 They did not get on very fast at this rate , but presently they reached a flat place that in time of flood was full of water , but was now , in the height of summer , only a set of pools , each surrounded with a fringe of plants , with bulrushes standing in the inside of all .
6 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
7 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the water and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?
8 Let them come and get the roots for themselves ; but we will fish for eels and anything else we can get . '
9 This delighted the rest of the party , and they all began to arrange their fishing lines , made from the bark of the yellow mimosa , and to search for bait for their hooks .
10 Most of them used worms , but one , who had put a piece of raw meat for dinner into his skin wallet , cut off a little bit and baited his line with it , unseen by his companions .
11 For a long time they cast patiently , without receiving a single bite ; the sun had grown low in the sky , and it seemed as if they would have to go home empty-handed , not even with a basket of roots to show ; when the youth , who had baited his hook with raw meat , suddenly saw his line disappear under the water .
12 Something , a very heavy fish he supposed , was pulling so hard that he could hardly keep his feet , and for a few minutes it seemed either as if he must let go or be dragged into the pool .
13 He cried to his friends to help him , and at last , trembling with fright at what they were going to see , they managed between them to land on the bank a creature that was neither a calf nor a seal , but something of both , with a long , broad tail .
14 They looked at each other with horror , cold shivers running down their spines ; for though they had never beheld it , there was not a man amongst them who did not know what it was -- the cub of the awful Bunyip !
15 All of a sudden the silence was broken by a low wail , answered by another from the other side of the pool , as the mother rose up from her den and came towards them , rage flashing from her horrible yellow eyes .
16 ` Let it go !
17 let it go ! '
18 whispered the young men to each other ; but the captor declared that he had caught it , and was going to keep it .
19 ` He had promised his sweetheart , ' he said , ` that he would bring back enough meat for her father 's house to feast on for three days , and though they could not eat the little Bunyip , her brothers and sisters should have it to play with . '
20 So , flinging his spear at the mother to keep her back , he threw the little Bunyip on to his shoulders , and set out for the camp , never heeding the poor mother 's cries of distress .
21 By this XXXXX it was getting near sunset , and the plain was in shadow , though the tops of the mountains were still quite bright .	time		basket|calf|camp|horror|mother|place|rage|side|time|youth

1 ` Indian Folklore . ' -RSB-
2 The Bunyip Long , long ago , far , far away on the other side of the world , some young men left the camp where they lived to get some food for their wives and children .
3 The sun was hot , but they liked heat , and as they went they ran races and tried who could hurl his spear the farthest , or was cleverest in throwing a strange weapon called a boomerang , which always returns to the thrower .
4 They did not get on very fast at this rate , but presently they reached a flat place that in time of flood was full of water , but was now , in the height of summer , only a set of pools , each surrounded with a fringe of plants , with bulrushes standing in the inside of all .
5 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
6 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the water and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?
7 Let them come and get the roots for themselves ; but we will fish for eels and anything else we can get . '
8 This delighted the rest of the party , and they all began to arrange their fishing lines , made from the bark of the yellow mimosa , and to search for bait for their hooks .
9 Most of them used worms , but one , who had put a piece of raw meat for dinner into his skin wallet , cut off a little bit and baited his line with it , unseen by his companions .
10 For a long time they cast patiently , without receiving a single bite ; the sun had grown low in the sky , and it seemed as if they would have to go home empty-handed , not even with a basket of roots to show ; when the youth , who had baited his hook with raw meat , suddenly saw his line disappear under the water .
11 Something , a very heavy fish he supposed , was pulling so hard that he could hardly keep his feet , and for a few minutes it seemed either as if he must let go or be dragged into the pool .
12 He cried to his friends to help him , and at last , trembling with fright at what they were going to see , they managed between them to land on the bank a creature that was neither a calf nor a seal , but something of both , with a long , broad tail .
13 They looked at each other with horror , cold shivers running down their spines ; for though they had never beheld it , there was not a man amongst them who did not know what it was -- the cub of the awful Bunyip !
14 All of a sudden the silence was broken by a low wail , answered by another from the other side of the pool , as the mother rose up from her den and came towards them , rage flashing from her horrible yellow eyes .
15 ` Let it go !
16 let it go ! '
17 whispered the young men to each other ; but the captor declared that he had caught it , and was going to keep it .
18 ` He had promised his sweetheart , ' he said , ` that he would bring back enough meat for her father 's house to feast on for three days , and though they could not eat the little Bunyip , her brothers and sisters should have it to play with . '
19 So , flinging his spear at the mother to keep her back , he threw the little Bunyip on to his shoulders , and set out for the camp , never heeding the poor mother 's cries of distress .
20 By this time it was getting near sunset , and the plain was in shadow , though the tops of the mountains were still quite bright .

3 In that country the people are fond of the roots of bulrushes , which they think as good as onions , and one of the young men said that they had better collect some of the roots and carry them back to the camp .
4 It did not take them long to weave the tops of the willows into a basket , and they were just going to wade into the water and pull up the bulrush roots when a youth suddenly called out : ` After all , why should we waste our time in doing work that is only fit for women and children ?
5 Let them come and get the roots for themselves ; but we will fish for eels and anything else we can get . '
6 This delighted the rest of the party , and they all began to arrange their fishing lines , made from the bark of the yellow mimosa , and to search for bait for their hooks .
7 Most of them used worms , but one , who had put a piece of raw meat for dinner into his skin wallet , cut off a little bit and baited his line with it , unseen by his companions .

11 They looked at each other with horror , cold shivers running down their spines ; for though they had never beheld it , there was not a man amongst them who did not know what it was -- the cub of the awful Bunyip !
12 All of a sudden the silence was broken by a low wail , answered by another from the other side of the pool , as the mother rose up from her den and came towards them , rage flashing from her horrible yellow eyes .
13 ` Let it go !
14 let it go ! '
15 whispered the young men to each other ; but the captor declared that he had caught it , and was going to keep it .
16 ` He had promised his sweetheart , ' he said , ` that he would bring back enough meat for her father 's house to feast on for three days , and though they could not eat the little Bunyip , her brothers and sisters should have it to play with . '
17 So , flinging his spear at the mother to keep her back , he threw the little Bunyip on to his shoulders , and set out for the camp , never heeding the poor mother 's cries of distress .
18 By this time it was getting near sunset , and the plain was in shadow , though the tops of the mountains were still quite bright .
19 The youths had all ceased to be afraid , when they were startled by a low rushing sound behind them , and , looking round , saw that the pool was slowly rising , and the spot where they had landed the Bunyip was quite covered .
20 ` What could it be ? '

1 For a long time they cast patiently , without receiving a single bite ; the sun had grown low in the sky , and it seemed as if they would have to go home empty-handed , not even with a basket of roots to show ; when the youth , who had baited his hook with raw meat , suddenly saw his line disappear under the water .
2 Something , a very heavy fish he supposed , was pulling so hard that he could hardly keep his feet , and for a few minutes it seemed either as if he must let go or be dragged into the pool .
3 He cried to his friends to help him , and at last , trembling with fright at what they were going to see , they managed between them to land on the bank a creature that was neither a calf nor a seal , but something of both , with a long , broad tail .
4 They looked at each other with horror , cold shivers running down their spines ; for though they had never beheld it , there was not a man amongst them who did not know what it was -- the cub of the awful Bunyip !
5 All of a sudden the silence was broken by a low wail , answered by another from the other side of the pool , as the mother rose up from her den and came towards them , rage flashing from her horrible yellow eyes .
6 ` Let it go !
7 let it go ! '
8 whispered the young men to each other ; but the captor declared that he had caught it , and was going to keep it .
9 ` He had promised his sweetheart , ' he said , ` that he would bring back enough meat for her father 's house to feast on for three days , and though they could not eat the little Bunyip , her brothers and sisters should have it to play with . '
10 So , flinging his spear at the mother to keep her back , he threw the little Bunyip on to his shoulders , and set out for the camp , never heeding the poor mother 's cries of distress .
11 By this time it was getting near sunset , and the plain was in shadow , though the tops of the mountains were still quite bright .
12 The youths had all ceased to be afraid , when they were startled by a low rushing sound behind them , and , looking round , saw that the pool was slowly rising , and the spot where they had landed the Bunyip was quite covered .
13 ` What could it be ? '
14 they asked one of another ; ` there was not a cloud in the sky , yet the water had risen higher already than they had ever known it do before . '
15 For an instant they stood watching as if they were frozen , then they turned and ran with all their might , the man with the Bunyip running faster than all .
16 When he reached a high peak over-looking all the plain he stopped to take breath , and turned to see if he was safe yet .
17 Safe !
18 why only the tops of the trees remained above that sea of water , and these were fast disappearing .
19 They must run fast indeed if they were to escape .
20 So on they flew , scarcely feeling the ground as they went , till they flung themselves on the ground before the holes scooped out of the earth where they had all been born .
21 The old XXXXX were sitting in front , the children were playing , and the women chattering together , when the little Bunyip fell into their midst , and there was scarcely a child among them who did not know that something terrible was upon them .	men		calf|creature|father|ground|men|shoulders|silence|sisters|something|sound

20 The old men were sitting in front , the children were playing , and the women chattering together , when the little Bunyip fell into their midst , and there was scarcely a child among them who did not know that something terrible was upon them .

1 ` He had promised his sweetheart , ' he said , ` that he would bring back enough meat for her father 's house to feast on for three days , and though they could not eat the little Bunyip , her brothers and sisters should have it to play with . '
2 So , flinging his spear at the mother to keep her back , he threw the little Bunyip on to his shoulders , and set out for the camp , never heeding the poor mother 's cries of distress .
3 By this time it was getting near sunset , and the plain was in shadow , though the tops of the mountains were still quite bright .
4 The youths had all ceased to be afraid , when they were startled by a low rushing sound behind them , and , looking round , saw that the pool was slowly rising , and the spot where they had landed the Bunyip was quite covered .
5 ` What could it be ? '
6 they asked one of another ; ` there was not a cloud in the sky , yet the water had risen higher already than they had ever known it do before . '
7 For an instant they stood watching as if they were frozen , then they turned and ran with all their might , the man with the Bunyip running faster than all .
8 When he reached a high peak over-looking all the plain he stopped to take breath , and turned to see if he was safe yet .
9 Safe !
10 why only the tops of the trees remained above that sea of water , and these were fast disappearing .
11 They must run fast indeed if they were to escape .
12 So on they flew , scarcely feeling the ground as they went , till they flung themselves on the ground before the holes scooped out of the earth where they had all been born .
13 The old men were sitting in front , the children were playing , and the women chattering together , when the little Bunyip fell into their midst , and there was scarcely a child among them who did not know that something terrible was upon them .
14 ` The water !
15 the water ! '
16 gasped one of the young men ; and there it was , slowly but steadily mounting the ridge itself .
17 Parents and children clung together , as if by that means they could drive back the advancing flood ; and the youth who had caused all this terrible catastrophe , seized his sweetheart , and cried : ' I will climb with you to the top of that tree , and there no waters can reach us . '
18 But , as he spoke , something cold touched him , and quickly he glanced down at his feet .
19 Then with a shudder he saw that they were feet no longer , but bird 's claws .
20 He looked at the girl he was clasping , and beheld a great black bird standing at his side ; he turned to his friends , but a flock of great awkward flapping creatures stood in their place He put up his hands to cover his face , but they were no more hands , only the ends of wings ; and when he tried to speak , a noise such as he had never heard before seemed to come from his throat , which had suddenly become narrow and slender .
21 Already the XXXXX had risen to his waist , and he found himself sitting easily upon it , while its surface reflected back the image of a black swan , one of many .	water		child|cloud|feet|front|girl|shoulders|time|water|waters|youth

1 By this time it was getting near sunset , and the plain was in shadow , though the tops of the mountains were still quite bright .
2 The youths had all ceased to be afraid , when they were startled by a low rushing sound behind them , and , looking round , saw that the pool was slowly rising , and the spot where they had landed the Bunyip was quite covered .
3 ` What could it be ? '
4 they asked one of another ; ` there was not a cloud in the sky , yet the water had risen higher already than they had ever known it do before . '
5 For an instant they stood watching as if they were frozen , then they turned and ran with all their might , the man with the Bunyip running faster than all .
6 When he reached a high peak over-looking all the plain he stopped to take breath , and turned to see if he was safe yet .
7 Safe !
8 why only the tops of the trees remained above that sea of water , and these were fast disappearing .
9 They must run fast indeed if they were to escape .
10 So on they flew , scarcely feeling the ground as they went , till they flung themselves on the ground before the holes scooped out of the earth where they had all been born .
11 The old men were sitting in front , the children were playing , and the women chattering together , when the little Bunyip fell into their midst , and there was scarcely a child among them who did not know that something terrible was upon them .
12 ` The water !
13 the water ! '
14 gasped one of the young men ; and there it was , slowly but steadily mounting the ridge itself .
15 Parents and children clung together , as if by that means they could drive back the advancing flood ; and the youth who had caused all this terrible catastrophe , seized his sweetheart , and cried : ' I will climb with you to the top of that tree , and there no waters can reach us . '
16 But , as he spoke , something cold touched him , and quickly he glanced down at his feet .
17 Then with a shudder he saw that they were feet no longer , but bird 's claws .
18 He looked at the girl he was clasping , and beheld a great black bird standing at his side ; he turned to his friends , but a flock of great awkward flapping creatures stood in their place He put up his hands to cover his face , but they were no more hands , only the ends of wings ; and when he tried to speak , a noise such as he had never heard before seemed to come from his throat , which had suddenly become narrow and slender .
19 Already the water had risen to his waist , and he found himself sitting easily upon it , while its surface reflected back the image of a black swan , one of many .
20 Never again did the swans become men ; but they are still different from other swans , for in the night-time those who listen can hear them talk in a language that is certainly not swan 's language ; and there are even sounds of laughing and talking , unlike any noise made by the swans whom we know .
21 The little Bunyip was carried home by its mother , and after that the XXXXX sank back to their own channels .	waters		Parents|man|midst|something|swan|top|tree|trees|waters|women
